10110{_}819{_}Once, about three years ago, and today, when I mentioned it to the police."
10110{_}820{_}The other man's sallow cheeks grew a shade more sallow.
10110{_}821{_}Grant supposed that this slight change of color indicated annoyance.
10110{_}822{_}Of course, the association of ideas in that curt answer was intolerably rude.
10110{_}823{_}But Grant had been tried beyond endurance that day.
10110{_}824{_}He was in a mood to be brusque with an archbishop.
10110{_}825{_}"We can disregard your confidences, or explanations, to the police," said Ingerman smoothly.
10110{_}826{_}"Three years ago, I suppose, my wife spoke of me?"
10110{_}827{_}"If you mean Miss Adelaide Melhuish--yes."
10110{_}828{_}"I do mean her.
10110{_}829{_}To be exact, I mean the lady who was murdered outside this house last night."
10110{_}830{_}Grant realized instantly that Isidor G. Ingerman was a foeman worthy of even a novelist's skill in repartee.
10110{_}831{_}Thus far, he, Grant, had been merely uncivil, using a bludgeon for wit, whereas the visitor was making play with a finely-tempered rapier.
10110{_}832{_}"Now that you have established your identity, Mr. Ingerman, perhaps you will tell me why you are here," he said.
10110{_}833{_}"I have come to Steynholme to inquire into my wife's death."
10110{_}834{_}"A most laudable purpose.
10110{_}835{_}I was given to understand, however, that at one time you took little interest in her living.
10110{_}836{_}I have not seen Mrs. Ingerman for three years--until last night, that is--so there is a chance, of course, that husband and wife may have adjusted their differences.
10110{_}837{_}Is that so?"
10110{_}838{_}"Until last night!"
10110{_}839{_}repeated Ingerman, almost in a startled tone.
10110{_}840{_}"You admit that?"
10110{_}841{_}Grant turned and pointed.
10110{_}842{_}"I saw, or fancied I saw, her face at that window," he said.
10110{_}843{_}"She looked in on me about ten minutes to eleven.
10110{_}844{_}I was hard at work, but the vision, as it seemed then, was so weird and unexpected, that I went straight out and searched for her.
10110{_}845{_}Perhaps 'searched' is not quite the right word.
10110{_}846{_}To be exact, I opened the French window, stood there, and listened.
10110{_}847{_}Then I persuaded myself that I was imagining a vain thing, and came in."
10110{_}848{_}"What was she doing here?"
10110{_}849{_}"I don't know."
10110{_}850{_}"She arrived in Steynholme on Sunday evening, I am told."
10110{_}851{_}"I heard that, too."
10110{_}852{_}"You imply that you did not meet her?"
10110{_}853{_}"No need to imply anything, Mr. Ingerman.
10110{_}854{_}I did not meet her.
10110{_}855{_}Beyond the fanciful notion that I had seen her ghost last night, the first I knew of her presence in the village was when I recognized her dead body this morning."
10110{_}856{_}"Strange as it may sound, I am inclined to believe you."
10110{_}857{_}Grant said nothing.
10110{_}858{_}He wanted to get up and pitch Ingerman into the road.
10110{_}859{_}"But who else will take that charitable view?"
10110{_}860{_}purred the other, in that suave voice which so ill accorded with his thin lips and slightly hooked nose.
10110{_}861{_}"I really don't care," was the weary answer.
10110{_}862{_}"Not at the moment, perhaps.
10110{_}863{_}You have had a trying day, no doubt.
10110{_}864{_}My visit at its close cannot be helpful.
10110{_}865{_}But--"  "I am feeling rather tired mentally," interrupted Grant, "so you will oblige me by not raising too many points at once.
10110{_}866{_}Why should you imagine that conversation with you in particular should add to my supposed distress?"
10110{_}867{_}"Doesn't it?"
10110{_}868{_}"No."
10110{_}869{_}"Why, then, may I ask, do you so obviously resent my questions?
10110{_}870{_}Who has so much right to put them as I?"
10110{_}871{_}Grant found that he must bestir himself.
10110{_}872{_}Thus far, the honors lay with this rather sinister-looking yet quiet-mannered visitor.
10110{_}873{_}"I am sorry if anything I have said lends color to that belief," he answered.
10110{_}874{_}"Candidly, I began by assuming that you forfeited any legal right years ago to interfere in behalf of Miss Melhuish, living or dead.
10110{_}875{_}Let us, at least, be candid with each other.
10110{_}876{_}Miss Melhuish herself told me that you and she had separated by mutual consent."
10110{_}877{_}"Allow me to emulate your candor.
10110{_}878{_}The actual fact is that you weaned my wife's affections from me."
10110{_}879{_}"That is a downright lie," said Grant coolly.
10110{_}880{_}Ingerman's peculiar temperament permitted him to treat this grave insult far more lightly than Grant's harmless, if irritating, reference to the police.
10110{_}881{_}"Let us see just what 'a lie' signifies," he said, almost judicially.
10110{_}882{_}"If a lady deserts her husband, and there is good reason to suspect that she is, in popular phrase, 'carrying on' with another man, how can the husband be lying if he charges that man with being the cause of the domestic upheaval?"
10110{_}883{_}"In this instance a hypothetical case is not called for.
10110{_}884{_}Three years ago, Mr. Ingerman, you had parted from your wife.
10110{_}885{_}Your name was never mentioned.
10110{_}886{_}Apparently, none in my circle had even heard of you.
10110{_}887{_}Miss Melhuish had won repute as a celebrated actress.
10110{_}888{_}I met her, in a sense, professionally.
10110{_}889{_}We became friends.
10110{_}890{_}I fancied I was in love with her.
10110{_}891{_}I proposed marriage.
10110{_}892{_}Then, and not until then, did the ghost of Mr."--Grant bent forward, and consulted the card--"Mr. Isidor G. Ingerman intrude."
10110{_}893{_}"So marriage was out of the question?"
10110{_}894{_}"If you expect an answer--yes."
10110{_}895{_}Ingerman rested the handle of his stick against his lips.
10110{_}896{_}"That isn't how the situation was represented to me at the time," he said thoughtfully.
10110{_}897{_}Grant was still sore with the recollection of the way in which the superintendent of police had forced him to confess the pitiful scheme whereby a woman in love had sought to gain her ends.
10110{_}898{_}He refused to sully her memory a second time that day, even to gain the upper hand in this troublesome controversy.
10110{_}899{_}"I neither know nor care what representations may have been made to you," he retorted.
10110{_}900{_}"I merely tell you the literal truth."
10110{_}901{_}"Possibly.
10110{_}902{_}Possibly.
10110{_}903{_}It was not I who used the word 'lie,' remember.
10110{_}904{_}But if you are ungracious enough to refuse to withdraw the offensive phrase, let it pass.
10110{_}905{_}We are not in France.
10110{_}906{_}This deadly business will be fought out in the law courts.
10110{_}907{_}I am here to-night of my own initiative.
10110{_}908{_}I thought it only fair and reasonable that you and I should meet before we are brought face to face at a coroner's inquest, and, it may be, in an Assize Court.... No, no, Mr. Grant.
10110{_}909{_}Pray do not put the worst construction on my words.
10110{_}910{_}_Someone_ murdered my wife.
10110{_}911{_}If the police show intelligence and reasonable skill, _someone_ will be tried for the crime.
10110{_}912{_}You and I will certainly be witnesses.
10110{_}913{_}That is what I meant to convey.
10110{_}914{_}The doubt in my mind was this--whether to be actively hostile or passively friendly to the man who, next to me, was interested in the poor woman now lying dead in a wretched stable of this village."
10110{_}915{_}The almost diabolical cleverness of this long speech, delivered without heat and with singularly adroit stress on various passages, was revealed by its effect on Grant.
10110{_}916{_}He was at once infuriated and puzzled.
10110{_}917{_}Ingerman was playing him as a fisherman humors a well-hooked salmon.
10110{_}918{_}The simile actually occurred to him, and he resolved to precipitate matters by coming straightway to the landing-net.
10110{_}919{_}"Is your friendship purchasable?"
10110{_}920{_}he inquired, making the rush without further preamble.
10110{_}921{_}"My wife was, I was led to believe," came the calm retort.
10110{_}922{_}Grant threw scruples to the wind now.
10110{_}923{_}Adelaide Mulhuish was being defamed, not by him, but by her husband.
10110{_}924{_}"We are at cross purposes," he said, weighing each word.
10110{_}925{_}"Your wife, who knew your character fairly well, I am convinced, thought that you were open to receive a cash consideration for your connivance in a divorce."
10110{_}926{_}"She had told me plainly that she would never live with me again.
10110{_}927{_}I was too fair-minded a man to place obstacles in the way when she wished to regain her freedom."
10110{_}928{_}"So it was true, then.
10110{_}929{_}What was the price?
10110{_}930{_}One thousand--two?
10110{_}931{_}I am not a millionaire."
10110{_}932{_}"Nor am I.
10110{_}933{_}As a mere matter of pounds, shillings, and pence, it was a serious matter for me when my wife's earnings ceased to come into the common stock."
10110{_}934{_}"My first, if rather vague, estimate of you was the correct one.
10110{_}935{_}You are a good bit of a scoundrel, and, if I guess rightly, a would-be blackmailer."
10110{_}936{_}"You are talking at random, Mr. Grant.
10110{_}937{_}The levying of blackmail connotes that the person bled desires that some discreditable, or dangerous, fact should be concealed."
10110{_}938{_}"Such is not my position."
10110{_}939{_}"I--I wonder."
10110{_}940{_}"I can relieve you of any oppressive doubt.
10110{_}941{_}I informed the police some few hours ago that you have appeared already in a similar role."
10110{_}942{_}"Oh, you did, did you?"
10110{_}943{_}snarled Ingerman, suddenly abandoning his pose, and gazing at Grant with a curiously snakelike glint in his black eyes.
10110{_}944{_}"Yes.
10110{_}945{_}It interested them, I fancied."
10110{_}946{_}Grant was sure of his man now, and rather relieved that the battle of wits was turning in his favor.
10110{_}947{_}"So you have begun already to scheme your defense?"
10110{_}948{_}"Hadn't you better go?"
10110{_}949{_}was the contemptuous retort.
10110{_}950{_}"You refuse to answer any further questions?"
10110{_}951{_}"I refuse to buy your proffered friendship--whatever that may mean."
10110{_}952{_}"Have I offered to sell it?"
10110{_}953{_}"I gathered as much."
10110{_}954{_}Ingerman rose.
10110{_}955{_}He was still master of himself, though his lanky body was taut with rage.
10110{_}956{_}He spoke calmly and with remarkable restraint.
10110{_}957{_}"Go through what I have said, and discover, if you can, the slightest hint of any suggested condonation of your offenses, whether avowed or merely suspected.
10110{_}958{_}I shall prove beyond dispute that you came between me and my wife.
10110{_}959{_}Don't hug the delusion that your three years' limit will save you.
10110{_}960{_}It will not.
10110{_}961{_}I wish you well of your attempt to prove that I was a consenting party to divorce proceedings.
10110{_}962{_}I came here to look you over.
10110{_}963{_}I have done so, and have arrived at a very definite opinion.
10110{_}964{_}I, also, have been interviewed by the police, and any unfavorable views they may have formed concerning me as the outcome of your ex parte statements are more than counteracted by the ugly facts of a ghastly murder.
10110{_}965{_}You were here shortly before eleven o'clock last night.
10110{_}966{_}My wife was here, too, and alive.
10110{_}967{_}This morning she was found dead, by you.
10110{_}968{_}At eleven o'clock last night I was playing bridge with three city men in my flat.
10110{_}969{_}When the news of the murder reached me to-day my first thought, after the shock of it had passed, was:--'That fellow, Grant, may be innocently involved in a terrible crime, and I may figure as the chief witness against him.'
10110{_}970{_}I am not speaking idly, as you will learn to your cost.
10110{_}971{_}Yet, when I come on an errand of mercy, you have the impudence to charge me with blackmail.
10110{_}972{_}You are in for a great awakening.
10110{_}973{_}Be sure of that!"
10110{_}974{_}And Isidor G. Ingerman walked out, leaving Grant uncomfortably aware that he had not seen the last of an implacable and bitter enemy.
10110{_}975{_}It was something new and very disturbing for a writer to find himself in the predicament of a man with an absolutely clear conscience yet perilously near the meshes of the criminal law.
10110{_}976{_}He had often analyzed such a situation in his books, but fiction diverged so radically from hard fact that the sensation was profoundly disconcerting, to say the least.
10110{_}977{_}He did not go to the post office.
10110{_}978{_}He was not equal to any more verbal fire-works that evening.
10110{_}979{_}So he lit a pipe, and reviewed Ingerman's well-rounded periods very carefully, even taking the precaution to jot down exact, phrases.
10110{_}980{_}He analyzed them, and saw that they were capable of two readings.
10110{_}981{_}Of course, it could not be otherwise.
10110{_}982{_}The plausible rascal must have conned them over until this essential was secured.
10110{_}983{_}Grant even went so far as to give them a grudging professional tribute.
10110{_}984{_}They held a canker of doubt, too, which it was difficult to dissect.
10110{_}985{_}Their veiled threats were perplexing.
10110{_}986{_}While their effect, as apart from literal significance, was fresh in his mind, he made a few notes of different interpretations.
10110{_}987{_}He went to bed rather early, but could not sleep until the small hours.
10110{_}988{_}Probably his rest, such as it was, would have been even more disturbed had he been able to accompany Ingerman to the Hare and Hounds Inn.
10110{_}989{_}A small but select company had gathered in the bar parlor.
10110{_}990{_}The two hours between eight and ten were the most important of the day to the landlord, Mr. Tomlin.
10110{_}991{_}It was then that he imparted and received the tit-bits of local gossip garnered earlier, the process involving a good deal of play with shining beer-handles and attractively labeled bottles.
10110{_}992{_}But this was a special occasion.
10110{_}993{_}Never before had there been a Steynholme murder before the symposium.
10110{_}994{_}Hitherto, such a grewsome topic was supplied, for the most part, by faraway London.
10110{_}995{_}To-night the eeriness and dramatic intensity of a notable crime lay at the very doors of the village.
10110{_}996{_}So Tomlin was more portentous than usual; Hobbs, the butcher, more assertive, Elkin, the "sporty" breeder of polo ponies, more inclined to "lay odds" on any conceivable subject, and Siddle, the chemist, a reserved man at the best, even less disposed to voice a definite opinion.
10110{_}997{_}Elkin was about twenty-five years of age, Siddle looked younger than his probable thirty-five years, while the others were on the stout and prosperous line of fifty.
10110{_}998{_}They were discussing the murder, of course, when Ingerman entered, and ordered a whiskey and soda.
10110{_}999{_}Instantly there was dead silence.
10110{_}1000{_}Looks and furtive winks were exchanged.
10110{_}1001{_}There had been talk of a detective being employed.
10110{_}1002{_}Perhaps this was he.
10110{_}1003{_}Mr. Tomlin knew the stranger's name, as he had taken a room, but that was the extent of the available information.
10110{_}1004{_}"A fine evenin', sir," said Tomlin, drawing a cork noisily.
10110{_}1005{_}"Looks as though we were in for a spell o' settled weather."
10110{_}1006{_}"Yes," agreed Ingerman, summing up the conclave at a glance.
10110{_}1007{_}"Somehow, such a lovely night ill accords with the cause of my visit to Steynholme."
10110{_}1008{_}"In-deed, sir?"
10110{_}1009{_}"Well, you and these other gentlemen may judge for yourselves.
10110{_}1010{_}It will be no secret tomorrow.
10110{_}1011{_}I am the husband of the lady who was found in the river outside Mr. Grant's residence this morning."
10110{_}1012{_}Sensation, as the descriptive reporters put it.
10110{_}1013{_}Mr. Tomlin was dumbly but unanimously elected chairman of the meeting, and was vaguely aware of his responsibilities.
10110{_}1014{_}He drew himself a fresh glass of bitter.
10110{_}1015{_}"You don't tell me, sir!"
10110{_}1016{_}he gasped.
10110{_}1017{_}"Well, the idee!
10110{_}1018{_}The pore lady's letters were addressed to Miss Adelaide Melhuish.
10110{_}1019{_}Perhaps you don't know, sir, that she stayed here!"
10110{_}1020{_}"Oh, yes.
10110{_}1021{_}I was told that by the local police-constable.
10110{_}1022{_}Have I, by any chance, been given her room?"
10110{_}1023{_}"No, sir.
10110{_}1024{_}Not likely.
10110{_}1025{_}It's locked, and the police have the key till the inquest is done with."
10110{_}1026{_}"As for the name," explained Ingerman, in his suave voice, "that was a mere stage pseudonym, an adopted name.
10110{_}1027{_}My wife was a famous actress, and there is a sort of tacit agreement that a lady in the theatrical profession shall be known to the public as 'Miss' rather than 'Mrs.'"
10110{_}1028{_}"Well, there!"
10110{_}1029{_}wheezed Tomlin.
10110{_}1030{_}"Who'd ever ha' thought it?"
10110{_}1031{_}The landlord was not quite rising to the occasion.
10110{_}1032{_}He was, in fact, stunned by these repeated shocks.
10110{_}1033{_}So Hobbs took charge.
10110{_}1034{_}"It's a sad errand you're on, sir," he said.
10110{_}1035{_}"Death comes to all of us, man an' beast alike, but it's a terrible thing when a lady like Miss-- Mrs. ----"  "Ingerman is my name, but my wife will certainly be alluded to by the press as Miss Melhuish."
10110{_}1036{_}"When a lady like Miss Melhuish is knocked on the 'ead like a--"  Mr. Hobbs hesitated again.
10110{_}1037{_}He also felt that the situation was rather beyond him.
10110{_}1038{_}"But my wife was flung into the river and drowned," said Ingerman sadly.
10110{_}1039{_}"No, sir.
10110{_}1040{_}She was killed fust.
10110{_}1041{_}It was a brutal business, so I'm told."
10110{_}1042{_}"Do you mean that she was struck, her skull battered?"
10110{_}1043{_}came the demand, in an awed and soul-thrilling whisper.
10110{_}1044{_}"Yes, sir.
10110{_}1045{_}An' the wust thing is, none of us can guess who could ha' done it."
10110{_}1046{_}"Lay yer five quid to one, Hobbs, that the police cop the scoundrel afore this day fortnight," cried Elkin noisily.
10110{_}1047{_}Then Mr. Siddle put in a mild word.
10110{_}1048{_}"Gentlemen," he said, "let me remind you that we four will probably be jurors at the inquest."
10110{_}1049{_}That was a sobering thought.
10110{_}1050{_}Elkin subsided, and Hobbs looked critically at the remains of a gill of beer.
10110{_}1051{_}Ingerman took stock of the chemist.
10110{_}1052{_}He might easily induce the others to believe that Grant was the real criminal, but the quiet man in the black morning-coat and striped cloth trousers was of finer metal.
10110{_}1053{_}He knew instantly that if he could persuade this one "probable juror" of Grant's guilt, the remainder would follow his lead like a flock of sheep.
10110{_}1054{_}But there was no need to hurry.
10110{_}1055{_}Next day's inquest would be a mere formality.
10110{_}1056{_}The real struggle would begin a week or a fortnight later.
10110{_}1057{_}"You have said a very wise thing, sir," he murmured appreciatively.
10110{_}1058{_}"Even my feelings must be kept under better control.
10110{_}1059{_}But this is no ordinary murder.
10110{_}1060{_}Before it is cleared up there will be astounding revelations.
10110{_}1061{_}Mark the word--astounding."
10110{_}1062{_}Hobbs, whose heavy cheeks were of a brick-red tint, almost startled the conclave by a sudden outburst which gave him an apoplectic appearance.
10110{_}1063{_}"You're too kind'earted, Siddle," he cried.
10110{_}1064{_}"Wot's the use of talkin' rubbish.
10110{_}1065{_}We all know where the body was found.
10110{_}1066{_}We all know that Doris Martin an' Mr. Grant were a'sweet-'eartin' in the garden--"  "Look here, Hobbs, just keep Doris Martin's name out of it!"
10110{_}1067{_}shouted Elkin, smiting the table with his fist till the glasses danced.
10110{_}1068{_}"Gentlemen!"
10110{_}1069{_}protested Siddle gently.
10110{_}1070{_}"It's all dashed fine, but I'm not--" blustered Elkin.
10110{_}1071{_}He yielded to Ingerman's outstretched hand.
10110{_}1072{_}"I seem to have brought discord into a friendly gathering," came the mournful comment.
10110{_}1073{_}"Such was far from being my intent.
10110{_}1074{_}Landlord, the round is on me, with cigars.
10110{_}1075{_}Now, let us talk of anything but this horror.
10110{_}1076{_}If I forget myself again, pull me up short, and fine me another round."
10110{_}1077{_}Siddle half rose, but thought better of it.
10110{_}1078{_}Evidently, he meant to use his influence to stop foolish chatter.
10110{_}1079{_}CHAPTER V  THE SEEDS OP MISCHIEF   Ingerman was a shrewder judge of human nature than the village chemist.
10110{_}1080{_}As well try to stem the flowing tide as stop tongues from wagging when such a theme offered.
10110{_}1081{_}Tomlin created a momentary diversion by clattering in the bar.
10110{_}1082{_}After this professional interlude, Ingerman ignored his own compact.
10110{_}1083{_}"I'm sure you local residents will be interested, at least, in hearing something of my wife's career," he said.
10110{_}1084{_}"There never was a more lovable and gracious woman, and no couple could be more united than she and I till some three years ago.
10110{_}1085{_}Then came a break.
10110{_}1086{_}She was independent of me, of course.
10110{_}1087{_}She was a celebrity, I a mere nobody, best known, if at all, as 'Miss Melhuish's husband.'
10110{_}1088{_}Nevertheless, we were devoted to each other until, to her and my lasting misfortune, a certain author wrote a book which, when dramatized, contained a part for which my wife's stage presence and talents seemed to be peculiarly suited."
10110{_}1089{_}Siddle stirred uneasily, but the others were still as partridges in stubble.
10110{_}1090{_}Ingerman did not intend to alarm the shy bird of the covey, however.
10110{_}1091{_}"I name no names," he said solemnly.
10110{_}1092{_}"Nor am I telling you anything that will not be thoroughly exposed before the coroner and elsewhere.
10110{_}1093{_}From that unhappy period dated our estrangement.
10110{_}1094{_}My wife fell under a fatal influence which lasted, practically unchecked, until the day, if not the very hour, of her death.
10110{_}1095{_}Do I blame her?
10110{_}1096{_}No--a thousand times no!
10110{_}1097{_}You see me, a plain man, considerably her senior.
10110{_}1098{_}_I_ had not the gift of writing impassioned love passages in which she could display her artistic genius.
10110{_}1099{_}When I came home from the City, tired after the day's work, _she_ was just beginning hers.
10110{_}1100{_}You know what London fashionable life is--the theater, a supper, a dance, some great lady's 'reception,' and the rest of it.
10110{_}1101{_}Ah, me!
10110{_}1102{_}The stage, and literature, and the arts generally are not for poor fellows moiling in a City office.
10110{_}1103{_}You gentlemen, I take it, are all happily married--"  "I'm not," said Elkin, "but I'll lay you long odds I will be soon."
10110{_}1104{_}For some reason, this remark produced a certain uneasiness among his friends.
10110{_}1105{_}Tomlin stared at the ash of one of the cigars "stood" by this talkative Londoner; Hobbs, whose glass had reached a low level again, examined the dregs almost fiercely; and Siddle seemed to be about to say something, but, with his usual restraint, kept silent.
10110{_}1106{_}Then Ingerman made a very shrewd guess, and wondered who Doris Martin was, and what Hobbs's cryptic allusion had meant.
10110{_}1107{_}"Good luck to you, sir," he said, "but--take no offense--don't marry an actress.
10110{_}1108{_}There's an old adage, 'Birds of a feather flock together.'
10110{_}1109{_}I would go farther, and interpolate the word 'should.'
10110{_}1110{_}If Adelaide Melhuish had never met me, but had married the man who could write her plays, this tragedy in real life would never have been."
10110{_}1111{_}"D--n him," muttered Elkin fiercely.
10110{_}1112{_}"He's done for now, anyhow.
10110{_}1113{_}He'll turn no more girls' heads for a bit."
10110{_}1114{_}"An' five minutes since you yapped at me like a vicious fox-terrier for 'intin' much the same thing," chortled Hobbs.
10110{_}1115{_}Siddle stood up.
10110{_}1116{_}"You ain't goin', Mr.
10110{_}1117{_}Siddle?"
10110{_}1118{_}went on the butcher.
10110{_}1119{_}"It's 'ardly 'arf past nine."
10110{_}1120{_}"I have some accounts to get out.
10110{_}1121{_}It's near the half year, you know," and Siddle vanished unobtrusively.
10110{_}1122{_}Hobbs shook his head, and gazed at Elkin as though the latter was a refractory bullock.
10110{_}1123{_}"Siddle's a fair-minded chap," he said.
10110{_}1124{_}"He can't stand 'earin' any of us 'angin' a man without a fair trial."
10110{_}1125{_}Ingerman had marked the chemist for more subtle treatment when an opportunity arose, or could be made.
10110{_}1126{_}At present, he was not sorry such a restraining influence was removed.
10110{_}1127{_}The next half hour should prove a golden one if well utilized.
10110{_}1128{_}He was right.
10110{_}1129{_}Before the inn was cleared, what between Elkin's savage comments and the other men's thinly-veiled allusions, he knew all that Steynholme could tell with regard to Grant and Doris Martin.
10110{_}1130{_}Grant's first thought next morning was of the girl who had been thrust so prominently into his life by the death of another woman.
10110{_}1131{_}That was, perhaps, the strangest outcome of the tragedy.
10110{_}1132{_}Doris was easily the prettiest and most intelligent girl in the village, a rare combination in itself, even among young ladies of much higher social position than a postmaster's daughter.
10110{_}1133{_}But her father was a self-educated man, whose life had been given to books, whose only hobby was the culture and study of bees.
10110{_}1134{_}He had often refused promotion, solely because his duties at Steynholme were light, and permitted of many free hours.
10110{_}1135{_}In his only child he found a quick pupil and a sympathetic helper.
10110{_}1136{_}Of her own accord she took to poetry and music.
10110{_}1137{_}In effect, had Doris Martin attended the best of boarding-schools and training colleges, she would have received a smattering of French and a fair knowledge of the piano or violin, whereas, after more humble tuition, it might fairly be said of her that few girls of her age had read so many books and assimilated their contents so thoroughly.
10110{_}1138{_}From her mother she inherited her good looks and a small yearly income, just sufficient to maintain a better wardrobe than her father's salary would permit.
10110{_}1139{_}Grant, newly settled in Steynholme, found the postmaster and his daughter intellectually on a par with himself, and this claim could certainly not be made on behalf of the local "society" element.
10110{_}1140{_}The three became excellent friends.
10110{_}1141{_}Naturally, the young people spent a good deal of time together.
10110{_}1142{_}But there had been no love-making--not a hint or whisper of it!
10110{_}1143{_}And now, by cruel chance, their names were linked by scandal in its most menacing form, since there was no gainsaying the fact that Doris's star-gazing on that fatal Monday night was indissolubly bound up with the death of Adelaide Melhuish.
10110{_}1144{_}For the first time, then, the notion peeped up in Grant's mind that the whirligig of existence might see Doris his wife.
10110{_}1145{_}But the conceit resembled the Gorgon's teeth, which, when sown in the ground, sprang forth as armed men.
10110{_}1146{_}The very accident which revealed a not unpleasing possibility had established a grave obstacle in the way of its ultimate realization.
10110{_}1147{_}Already there was a cloud between him and the Martins, father and daughter.
10110{_}1148{_}To what a tempest might not that cloud develop when the questionings and innuendoes of the inquest established an aura of suspicion and intrigue around a perfectly innocent meeting in the garden of The Hollies!
10110{_}1149{_}Grant ate his breakfast in wrath.
10110{_}1150{_}In wrath, too, he glanced through the morning newspapers, and saw his own name figuring large in the "story" of the "alleged" murder.
10110{_}1151{_}The reporters had missed nothing.
10110{_}1152{_}They had even got hold of the "peculiar coincidence" of his (Grant's) glimpse of a face at the window.
10110{_}1153{_}His play was recalled, and Adelaide Melhuish's success in the title-role.
10110{_}1154{_}Then Mr. Isidor G. Ingerman was introduced.
10110{_}1155{_}He was described as "a man fairly well known in the City."
10110{_}1156{_}That was all.
10110{_}1157{_}The press could say nothing as yet of marital disagreements, nor was any hint concerning Doris Martin allowed to appear.
10110{_}1158{_}But these journalistic fire-works were only held in reserve.
10110{_}1159{_}"Dramatic and sensational developments" were promised, and police activity in "an unexpected direction" fore-shadowed.
10110{_}1160{_}All of which, of course, was mere journalistic paraphrasing of circumstances already known to the writers, and none the less galling to Grant on that account.
10110{_}1161{_}And there was no answer from the Commissioner of Police at Scotland Yard.
10110{_}1162{_}True, the overnight telegram might have reached the Department after office hours.
10110{_}1163{_}Grant, like most members of the general public, held the vague belief that Government officials do very little work.
10110{_}1164{_}Still, one might reasonably expect better things from the institution which was supposed to safeguard law-abiding citizens.
10110{_}1165{_}Calm analysis of Ingerman's nebulous threats had revealed a hostile force not to be despised.
10110{_}1166{_}Possibly, the man was already in league with that narrow-minded village constable, so every passing hour made more urgent the need of a trained intelligence being brought to bear on the mystery of Adelaide Melhuish's killing.
10110{_}1167{_}Grant racked his brains to discover who could possibly have a motive for committing the crime.
10110{_}1168{_}Naturally, his thoughts flew to Ingerman.
10110{_}1169{_}Surely that sinister-looking person should be forced to give an account of himself instead of, as was probable, being allowed to instill further nonsense into the suspicious mind of P.C.
10110{_}1170{_}Robinson.
10110{_}1171{_}There were two morning deliveries of London letters in Steynholme, one at eight and another at half past ten.
10110{_}1172{_}Grant waited until the postman had left a publisher's circular (the only letter for The Hollies by the second mail).
10110{_}1173{_}Then, in a fever of impatience, he jammed on a hat and went out.
10110{_}1174{_}He would wait no longer.
10110{_}1175{_}He would telegraph Scotland Yard again, and, incidentally, demand an audience at the post office.
10110{_}1176{_}No sooner had he entered the highroad than he saw P.C.
10110{_}1177{_}Robinson on guard.
10110{_}1178{_}That important person was standing on the bridge, apparently taking the air.
10110{_}1179{_}He was nibbling the chin-strap of his helmet; both thumbs were locked in his belt.
10110{_}1180{_}From that strategic position three roads came under observation.
10110{_}1181{_}It was a fine morning, and Grant's sense of humor was not proof against this open espionage.
10110{_}1182{_}He smiled, and determined to take a rise out of "Sherlock," as Bates had christened the policeman.
10110{_}1183{_}The bridge lay a hundred yards to the left.
10110{_}1184{_}The road was straight until it curved around the house and its shrubberies, so the view was blocked on that side.
10110{_}1185{_}Grant filled and lighted a pipe with a deliberateness meant to be provoking, glancing several times doubtfully at P.C.
10110{_}1186{_}Robinson, who, of course, was grandly unaware of his presence.
10110{_}1187{_}Then he strolled off to the right, and, when hidden, took to his heels for a hundred yards sprint.
10110{_}1188{_}Turning into a winding bridle-path tucked between hedges of thorn and hazels, he walked to a point where it crossed a patch of furze.
10110{_}1189{_}At a little distance a hand-bridge spanned the river, and gave access to the eastern end of the village by a steep climb of the wooded cliff.
10110{_}1190{_}The path, in fact, was a short cut to that part of Steynholme.
10110{_}1191{_}He sat on a hump of rock, and waited.
10110{_}1192{_}It was a boyish trick, but very successful.
10110{_}1193{_}Within three minutes, at the utmost, P.C.
10110{_}1194{_}Robinson hurried past, using a stalking, stealthy stride which was distinctly ludicrous.
10110{_}1195{_}The eyes of the two men met, but Grant alone was prepared.
10110{_}1196{_}"Hello, Robinson!"
10110{_}1197{_}he cried cheerfully.
10110{_}1198{_}"What's the rush?
10110{_}1199{_}Surely our rural peace has not been disturbed again?"
10110{_}1200{_}Robinson knew he had been "sold," but rose to the occasion.
10110{_}1201{_}"Excuse me, Mr. Grant," he puffed.
10110{_}1202{_}"Can't wait now.
10110{_}1203{_}Have an appointment.
10110{_}1204{_}I'll see you later."
10110{_}1205{_}Honor demanded that he should not relax that swift pace.
10110{_}1206{_}Unhappily, the path up the cliff was visible throughout from Grant's rock, so, on reaching the summit, Robinson was a-boil in more ways than one.
10110{_}1207{_}Moreover, peeping through the first screen of trees that offered, he had the mortification of seeing the man who had befooled him go back the way he came.
10110{_}1208{_}Purple-faced with heat and anger, the policeman forgot his surroundings, and glowered at Grant with real fury.
10110{_}1209{_}So he heard no one approaching along the main road until he was hailed a second time with, "Hello, Robinson!"
10110{_}1210{_}He turned sharply.
10110{_}1211{_}This was Mr. Elkin.
10110{_}1212{_}"Good morning!"
10110{_}1213{_}he said.
10110{_}1214{_}"Have you seen the superintendent?"
10110{_}1215{_}"What?
10110{_}1216{_}Mr. Fowler?
10110{_}1217{_}No.
10110{_}1218{_}Is _he_ here so early?"
10110{_}1219{_}"I must have missed him."
10110{_}1220{_}"Well, you'll hardly find him on Bush Walk," which was the name of the path.
10110{_}1221{_}"You never can tell," came the dark answer.
10110{_}1222{_}At any rate, the policeman elected to abandon his self-imposed vigil, and the two walked together into the village.
10110{_}1223{_}"My!
10110{_}1224{_}You look as though you'd run a mile," commented Elkin.
10110{_}1225{_}"This murder has kept me busy," growled the other, frankly mopping his forehead.
10110{_}1226{_}"Ay, that's so.
10110{_}1227{_}And it isn't done with yet, by a long way.
10110{_}1228{_}Pity you weren't in the Hare and Hounds last night.
10110{_}1229{_}You'd have heard something.
10110{_}1230{_}There's a chap staying there, name of Ingerman--"  "I've met him.
10110{_}1231{_}The dead woman's husband."
10110{_}1232{_}"Oh, perhaps you've got his yarn already?"
10110{_}1233{_}"It all depends what he said to you."
10110{_}1234{_}"Well, he hinted things.
10110{_}1235{_}Unless I'm greatly mistaken, you'll soon be making an arrest."
10110{_}1236{_}"I believe I could put my hand on the murderer this very minute," said Robinson vindictively.
10110{_}1237{_}Elkin laughed, somewhat half-heartedly.
10110{_}1238{_}"Lay you fifty to one against the time," he said.
10110{_}1239{_}"I'm the only one near enough for that limit, you know."
10110{_}1240{_}The policeman realized that he had allowed annoyance to shake his wits.
10110{_}1241{_}He looked at Elkin rather sharply, and noticed that the horse-breeder seemed to be nervous and ill.  "I didn't quite mean that I could grab my man this minute," he said, "but, if I can guess him, it amounts to nearly the same thing.
10110{_}1242{_}What have you been doing to yourself, Mr. Elkin?
10110{_}1243{_}You look peeky to-day."
10110{_}1244{_}"Too much whiskey and tobacco.
10110{_}1245{_}I'll call at Siddle's for a 'pick-me-up.'
10110{_}1246{_}Am I wanted for the jury?"
10110{_}1247{_}"Yes.
10110{_}1248{_}I left a notice at your place last evening."
10110{_}1249{_}"I didn't get it."
10110{_}1250{_}"Been away?"
10110{_}1251{_}"No.
10110{_}1252{_}Fact is, I went home late, and didn't bother about letters this morning.
10110{_}1253{_}What time is the inquest?"
10110{_}1254{_}"Three o'clock, in the club-room of the Hare and Hounds."
10110{_}1255{_}"Will that fellow, Grant, be there?"
10110{_}1256{_}"Rather.
10110{_}1257{_}Dr. Foxton warned him yesterday."
10110{_}1258{_}"Good!
10110{_}1259{_}What about Doris Martin?
10110{_}1260{_}Will she be a witness?"
10110{_}1261{_}"Not to-day."
10110{_}1262{_}They were entering the village, and could see down the long, wide slope of the hill.
10110{_}1263{_}Grant had just come into sight at its foot.
10110{_}1264{_}Both men scowled at the distant figure, but neither passed any comment.
10110{_}1265{_}They parted, the policeman walking straight on, Elkin bearing to the left.
10110{_}1266{_}The chemist's shop stood exactly opposite the post office, so Elkin, arriving first, was aware of his unconscious rival's destination.
10110{_}1267{_}He had not answered Mr. Siddle's greeting, but gazed moodily through a barricade of specifics piled in the window.
10110{_}1268{_}Then he swore.
10110{_}1269{_}"What's wrong now?"
10110{_}1270{_}inquired the chemist quietly.
10110{_}1271{_}"That Grant.
10110{_}1272{_}Got a nerve, hasn't he?"
10110{_}1273{_}"I can't say, unless you explain."
10110{_}1274{_}"He's just gone into the post office."
10110{_}1275{_}"Why shouldn't he?
10110{_}1276{_}He wants stamps, may be; plenty of 'em, I should imagine."
10110{_}1277{_}"Oh, you're a fish, Siddle.
10110{_}1278{_}You aren't crazy about a girl, like I am.
10110{_}1279{_}The sooner Grant's in jail the better I'll be pleased."
10110{_}1280{_}"If you take my advice, which you won't, I know, you will not utter that sort of remark publicly."
10110{_}1281{_}"Can't help it.
10110{_}1282{_}Bet you a fiver I'm engaged to Doris Martin within a week."
10110{_}1283{_}Mr. Siddle took thought.
10110{_}1284{_}"Why so quickly?"
10110{_}1285{_}he asked, after a pause.
10110{_}1286{_}"I'll catch her on the hop, of course.
10110{_}1287{_}If she's engaged to me it'll help her a lot when this case comes into court."
10110{_}1288{_}"I cannot believe that Doris would accept any man for such a reason."
10110{_}1289{_}"I'm not 'any man.'
10110{_}1290{_}She knows I'm after her.
10110{_}1291{_}Will you take my bet, even money?"
10110{_}1292{_}"No.
10110{_}1293{_}I don't bet."
10110{_}1294{_}"Well, you needn't put a damper on me.
10110{_}1295{_}In fact, you can't.
10110{_}1296{_}Have you that last prescription of Dr. Foxton's handy?
10110{_}1297{_}My liver wants a tonic."
10110{_}1298{_}The chemist thumbed a dog-eared volume, read an entry carefully, and retired to a dispensing counter in the rear of the shop.
10110{_}1299{_}"Shall I send it?"
10110{_}1300{_}came his voice.
10110{_}1301{_}"No.
10110{_}1302{_}I'll wait.
10110{_}1303{_}Give me a dose now, if you don't mind."
10110{_}1304{_}For some reason, Fred Elkin was not himself that day.
10110{_}1305{_}He was moody, and fretful as a sick colt.
10110{_}1306{_}But he had diagnosed his ailment and its cause accurately; a discreet doctor was probably aware of his failings, and had considered them in the "mixture."
10110{_}1307{_}The post office was not busy when Grant entered.
10110{_}1308{_}A young man, a stranger, was seated at the telegraphist's desk, tapping a new instrument.
10110{_}1309{_}The G. P. O., forewarned, had lent an expert to deal with press messages.
10110{_}1310{_}Mr. Martin, sorting some documents, came forward when he saw Grant.
10110{_}1311{_}His kindly, somewhat pre-occupied face was long as a fiddle.
10110{_}1312{_}"Good morning, Mr. Martin," said Grant.
10110{_}1313{_}"Good morning.
10110{_}1314{_}What can I do for you?"
10110{_}1315{_}was the stiff reply.
10110{_}1316{_}Grant was in no mind to be rebuffed, however.
10110{_}1317{_}"I must have a word with you in private," he said.
10110{_}1318{_}"I'm sorry--but my time is quite full."
10110{_}1319{_}"I'm sorry, too, but the matter is urgent."
10110{_}1320{_}The click of the sounder became less businesslike.
10110{_}1321{_}There was an element in the tone of each voice that drew the London telegraphist's attention.
10110{_}1322{_}Martin, usually the mildest-mannered man in Sussex, was obviously ill at ease.
10110{_}1323{_}But he simply could not hold out against Grant's compelling gaze.
10110{_}1324{_}"Come into the back room," he said nervously.
10110{_}1325{_}"Call me if I'm needed," he added, nodding to his assistant.
10110{_}1326{_}Grant did not hesitate an instant when the postmaster reached the "back parlor" through another door.
10110{_}1327{_}The open window, draped in clematis, gave a delightful glimpse of The Hollies.
10110{_}1328{_}A window-box of mignonette filled the air with its delicate perfume.
10110{_}1329{_}Grant hoped that Doris would be there, but the only signs of her recent presence were a hat and an open book on the table.
10110{_}1330{_}"Now, Mr. Martin," he said gravely, "you and I should have a serious talk.
10110{_}1331{_}It is idle to deny that gossip is spreading broadcast certain malicious and absurd rumors which closely concern Doris and myself.
10110{_}1332{_}To me these things are of slight consequence.
10110{_}1333{_}To a girl of your daughter's age they are poisonous.
10110{_}1334{_}If you, her father, know the whole truth, you can regulate your actions so as to defeat the scandalmongers.
10110{_}1335{_}That is why I am here to-day.
10110{_}1336{_}That is why I came here yesterday, but your attitude took me aback, and I was idiot enough to go without a word of explanation.
10110{_}1337{_}I was too shaken then to see my clear course, and follow it regardless of personal feelings.
10110{_}1338{_}This morning I am master of myself, and I insist that you listen now while I tell you exactly what occurred on Monday night."
10110{_}1339{_}"Surely--these matters--are--for the authorities," stammered the older man.
10110{_}1340{_}"What?
10110{_}1341{_}Your daughter's good name?"
10110{_}1342{_}Mr. Martin reddened.
10110{_}1343{_}His agitation was pitiful.
10110{_}1344{_}"That is hardly in question, sir," he said brokenly.
10110{_}1345{_}"I am speaking of the tongue of slander.
10110{_}1346{_}Heaven help and direct me!
10110{_}1347{_}I would suffer death rather than see Doris subjected to the leers and innuendoes of every lout in the village."
10110{_}1348{_}Grant's earnestness could hardly fail to impress his friend.
10110{_}1349{_}But Martin had either made up his mind or been warned not to discuss the murder, and adhered loyally to that line of conduct.
10110{_}1350{_}He retreated toward the door leading to the post office proper.
10110{_}1351{_}"It is too late to interfere now," he said.
10110{_}1352{_}"What on earth do you mean?"
10110{_}1353{_}demanded Grant, yielding to a gust of anger.
10110{_}1354{_}"The whole--of the circumstances--are being inquired into by the police," came the hesitating answer.
10110{_}1355{_}"Has that prying scoundrel, Robinson, dared to cross-examine Doris?"
10110{_}1356{_}"He came here, of course, but Scotland Yard has taken up the inquiry."
10110{_}1357{_}"A detective--here?"
10110{_}1358{_}"Yes.
10110{_}1359{_}He is with Doris in the garden at this moment."
10110{_}1360{_}Grant knew the topography of the house.
10110{_}1361{_}Without asking permission, he tore through yet a third door leading to a kitchen and scullery, nearly upsetting a tiny maid who had her ear or eye to the key-hole, and raced into the garden in which the postmaster kept his bees.
10110{_}1362{_}Doris, standing with her hands behind her back, was looking at The Hollies, and deep in conversation with an alert and natty little man who was evidently absorbed in what she was saying.
10110{_}1363{_}Grant, in a whirl of fury, was only conscious that Doris's companion was slight, almost diminutive, of frame, very erect, and dressed in a well-fitting blue serge suit, neat brown boots and straw hat, when the two heard his footsteps.
10110{_}1364{_}Doris was flustered.
10110{_}1365{_}Her Romney face held a look of scare.
10110{_}1366{_}"Oh, here is Mr.
10110{_}1367{_}Grant!"
10110{_}1368{_}she said, striving vainly to speak with composure.
10110{_}1369{_}The little man pierced Grant with an extraordinarily penetrating glance from very bright and deeply-recessed black eyes.
10110{_}1370{_}"Ah, Mr. Grant, is it!"
10110{_}1371{_}he chirped pleasantly.
10110{_}1372{_}"Good morning!
10110{_}1373{_}So _you're_ the villain of the piece, are you?"
10110{_}1374{_}CHAPTER VI  SCOTLAND YARD TAKES A HAND   It was a singular greeting, to say the least, and the person who uttered it was quite as remarkable as his queer method of expressing himself seemed to indicate.
10110{_}1375{_}Grant, though in a fume of hot anger, had the good sense to choke back the first impetuous reprimand trembling on his lips.
10110{_}1376{_}In fact, wrath quickly subsided into blank incredulity.
10110{_}1377{_}He saw before him, not the conventional detective who might be described as a superior Robinson--not even the sinewy, sharp-eyed, and well-spoken type of man whom he had once heard giving evidence in a famous jewel-robbery case--but rather one whom he would have expected to meet in the bar of a certain well-known restaurant in Maiden Lane, a corner of old London where literally all the world's a stage, and all the men and women merely players.
10110{_}1378{_}During his theatrical experiences he had come across scores of such men, dapper little fellows, wizened of face yet curiously youthful in manner; but they, each and all, were labeled "low comedian."
10110{_}1379{_}Certainly, a rare intelligence gleamed from this man's eyes, but that is an attribute not often lacking in humorists who command high salaries because of their facility in laughter-making.
10110{_}1380{_}This man, too, had the wide, thin-lipped, mobile mouth of the actor.
10110{_}1381{_}His ivory-white, wrinkled forehead and cheeks, the bluish tint on jaws and chin, his voice, his perky air, the very tilt of his straw hat, were eloquent of the footlights.
10110{_}1382{_}Even his opening words, bizarre and cheerfully impertinent, smacked of "comic relief."
10110{_}1383{_}"I figure prominently in this particular 'piece,'" snapped Grant.
10110{_}1384{_}"May I ask your name, sir?"
10110{_}1385{_}"A wise precaution with suspicious characters," rejoined the other, smiling.
10110{_}1386{_}Grant was suddenly reminded of a Japanese grinning at a joke, but he bent over a card which the stranger had whisked out of a waistcoat pocket.
10110{_}1387{_}He read:  MR. CHARLES F. FUENEAUX,  _Criminal Investigation Department_,  NEW SCOTLAND YARD, S.W.
10110{_}1388{_}He could not control himself.
10110{_}1389{_}He gazed at Mr. Charles F. Furneaux with a surprise that was not altogether flattering.
10110{_}1390{_}"Did the Commissioner of Police send _you_ in response to my telegram?"
10110{_}1391{_}he said.
10110{_}1392{_}"That is what lawyers call a leading question," came the prompt retort.
10110{_}1393{_}"And I hate lawyers.
10110{_}1394{_}They darken understanding, and set honest men at loggerheads."
10110{_}1395{_}"But it happens to be very much to the point at this moment."
10110{_}1396{_}"Well, Mr. Grant, if you really press for an answer, it is 'Yes' and 'No.'
10110{_}1397{_}The Commissioner received a certain telegram, but he may have acted on other grounds.
10110{_}1398{_}Even Commissioners can be creatures of impulse, or expediency, just as the situation demands.
10110{_}1399{_}"You are here, at any rate."
10110{_}1400{_}"That is what legal jargon terms an admitted fact."
10110{_}1401{_}"Then you had better begin by assuming that I am no villain."
10110{_}1402{_}"It is assumed.
10110{_}1403{_}It couldn't well be otherwise after the excellent character you have been given by this young lady."
10110{_}1404{_}"She, at least, will speak well of me, I do believe," said Grant, with a strange bitterness, for his heart was sore because of the seeming defection of his friend, the postmaster.
10110{_}1405{_}"What I actually had in mind was the stupidity of the local policeman, who is convinced that I am both a criminal and a fool."
10110{_}1406{_}"The two are often synonymous," said Furneaux dryly.
10110{_}1407{_}"But I acquitted you on both counts, Mr. Grant, on hearing, and even seeing, how you spent Monday evening."
10110{_}1408{_}Grant, who had cooled down considerably, found a hint of badinage in this comment.
10110{_}1409{_}"You have evidently been told that Miss Martin and I were star-gazing in the garden of my house," he said.
10110{_}1410{_}"It happens to be true."
10110{_}1411{_}"Oh, yes.
10110{_}1412{_}There was a very fine cluster of small stars in Canis Major, south of Sirius, that night."
10110{_}1413{_}"You know something about the constellations, then?"
10110{_}1414{_}was the astonished query.
10110{_}1415{_}"Enough for the purposes of Scotland Yard," smirked Furneaux, who had checked P.C.
10110{_}1416{_}Robinson's one-sided story by referring to Whitaker's Almanack.
10110{_}1417{_}"It may relieve your mind if I tell you that I have never seen a real live astronomer in the dock.
10110{_}1418{_}Venus and Mars are often in trouble, but their devoted observers seldom, if ever."
10110{_}1419{_}Grant warmed to this strange species of detective, though, if pressed for an instant decision, he would vastly have preferred that one of more orthodox style had been intrusted with an inquiry so vital to his own happiness and good repute.
10110{_}1420{_}Eager, however, to pour forth his worries into any official ear, he brought back the talk to a definite channel.
10110{_}1421{_}"Will you come to my place?"
10110{_}1422{_}he asked.
10110{_}1423{_}"I have much to say.
10110{_}1424{_}Let me assure you now, in Miss Martin's presence, that she is no more concerned in this ghastly business than any other young lady in the village."
10110{_}1425{_}"But she is interested.
10110{_}1426{_}And _you_ are.
10110{_}1427{_}And I am.
10110{_}1428{_}Why not discuss matters here, for the present, I mean?
10110{_}1429{_}We have a glorious view of your house and grounds.
10110{_}1430{_}We can see without being seen.
10110{_}1431{_}None can overhear.
10110{_}1432{_}I advise both of you to go thoroughly into this matter here and now."
10110{_}1433{_}Furneaux spoke emphatically.
10110{_}1434{_}Even Doris put in a timid plea.
10110{_}1435{_}"Perhaps that would be the best thing to do," she said.
10110{_}1436{_}"Mr. Furneaux has been most sympathetic.
10110{_}1437{_}I am sure he understands things already in a way that is quite wonderful to me."
10110{_}1438{_}The very sound of her voice was comforting.
10110{_}1439{_}Grant might have argued with the detective, but could not resist Doris.
10110{_}1440{_}Without further demur he went through the whole story, giving precise details of events on the Monday night.
10110{_}1441{_}Then the recital widened out into a history of his relations with Adelaide Melhuish.
10110{_}1442{_}He omitted nothing.
10110{_}1443{_}Doris gasped when she heard Superintendent Fowler's version of the view a coroner's jury might take of her presence in the garden of The Hollies at a late hour.
10110{_}1444{_}But Grant did not spare her.
10110{_}1445{_}He reasoned that she ought to be prepared for an ordeal which could not be avoided.
10110{_}1446{_}He was governed by the astute belief that his very outspokenness in this respect would weaken the inferences which the police might otherwise draw from it.
10110{_}1447{_}Furneaux uttered never a word.
10110{_}1448{_}He was a first-rate listener, though his behavior was most undetective-like, since he hardly looked at Grant or the girl, but seemed to devote his attention almost exclusively to the scenic panorama in front.
10110{_}1449{_}However, when Grant came to the somewhat strenuous passage-at-arms of the previous night between Ingerman and himself, the little man broke in at once.
10110{_}1450{_}"Isidor G.
10110{_}1451{_}Ingerman?"
10110{_}1452{_}he cried.
10110{_}1453{_}"Is he a tall, lanky, cadaverous, rather crooked person, with black hair turning gray, and an absurdly melodious voice?"
10110{_}1454{_}"You have described him without an unnecessary word," said Grant.
10110{_}1455{_}Furneaux clicked his tongue in a peculiar fashion.
10110{_}1456{_}"Go on!"
10110{_}1457{_}he said.
10110{_}1458{_}"It's a regular romance--quite in your line, Mr. Grant, of course, but none the less enthralling because, as you so happily phrased Miss Martin's lesson in astronomy, it happens to be true."
10110{_}1459{_}Grant was scrupulously fair to Ingerman.
10110{_}1460{_}He admitted the "financier's" adroitness of speech, and made clear the fact that if the visit had the levying of blackmail for its object such a possible outcome was only hinted at vaguely.
10110{_}1461{_}Being a novelist, one whose temperament sought for sunshine rather than gloom in life, he wound up in lighter vein.
10110{_}1462{_}The ruse which tricked P.C.
10110{_}1463{_}Robinson into a breathless scamper of nearly a mile on a hot day in June was described with gusto.
10110{_}1464{_}Doris, who knew the village constable well, laughed outright, while Furneaux cackled shrilly.
10110{_}1465{_}None who might be watching the little group in that delightful garden, with its scent of old-world flowers and drone of bees, could have guessed that a grewsome tragedy formed their major theme.
10110{_}1466{_}The girl was the first to realize that even harmless merriment was in ill accord with the presence of death, for the body of Adelaide Melhuish lay within forty yards of the place where they stood.
10110{_}1467{_}"May I leave you now?"
10110{_}1468{_}she inquired.
10110{_}1469{_}"Father may be wanting help in the office."
10110{_}1470{_}"I shan't detain you more than a few seconds," said Furneaux briskly.
10110{_}1471{_}"On Monday evening you two young people parted at half past ten.
10110{_}1472{_}How do you fix the time?"
10110{_}1473{_}Doris answered without hesitation:  "The large window of Mr. Grant's study was open, and we both heard a clock in the hall chime the half-hour.
10110{_}1474{_}I said, 'Goodness me, is that half past ten?'
10110{_}1475{_}and started for home at once.
10110{_}1476{_}Mr. Grant came with me as far as the bridge.
10110{_}1477{_}When I reached my room, in exactly five minutes after leaving The Hollies, I stood at the open window--that window"--and she pointed to a dormer casement above the sitting-room--"and looked out.
10110{_}1478{_}It was a particularly fine night, mild, but not very clear, as a slight mist often rises from the river after a hot day in summer.
10110{_}1479{_}I may have been there about ten minutes, no longer, when I saw the study window of The Hollies thrown open, and Mr. Grant's figure was silhouetted by the lamp behind him.
10110{_}1480{_}He seemed to be listening for something, so I, who must have heard any unusual sound, listened too.
10110{_}1481{_}There was nothing.
10110{_}1482{_}I could hear the ripple of the river beneath the bridge, so everything was very still.
10110{_}1483{_}After a minute, or two, perhaps--no longer--Mr. Grant went in, and closed the window.
10110{_}1484{_}Then I went to bed."
10110{_}1485{_}"Did Mr. Grant draw any blind or curtains?"
10110{_}1486{_}"There are muslin curtains attached to each side of the window.
10110{_}1487{_}One cannot see into the room from a distance."
10110{_}1488{_}Furneaux measured an imaginary line drawn from Doris's bedroom to the edge of the cliff, and prolonged it.
10110{_}1489{_}"Nor can you see the river or foot of the lawn from your room," he commented.
10110{_}1490{_}"No.
10110{_}1491{_}In winter I can just make out the edge of the lawn.
10110{_}1492{_}When the trees are in leaf, all the lower part is hidden."
10110{_}1493{_}"You had actually retired to rest about eleven, I suppose?"
10110{_}1494{_}"Yes."
10110{_}1495{_}"So if Mr. Grant came out again you would not know?"
10110{_}1496{_}Doris blushed furiously, but her reply was unfaltering.
10110{_}1497{_}"I would have known during the next half-hour, at least," she said.
10110{_}1498{_}"An inclined mirror hangs in my room.
10110{_}1499{_}I use it sometimes for adjusting a hat.
10110{_}1500{_}The square of light from Mr. Grant's room is reflected in it, and any sudden increase in the illumination caused by opening the window or pulling the curtains aside would certainly have caught my eye."
10110{_}1501{_}"You have an unshakable witness in Miss Martin," said Furneaux, stabbing a finger at Grant.
10110{_}1502{_}"Now, I'll hurry off.
10110{_}1503{_}You and I, Mr. Grant, meet at Philippi, otherwise known as the crowner's quest."
10110{_}1504{_}Any benevolent intent he may have had in leaving these young people together was, however, frustrated by Doris, whose composure seemed to have fled since her statement about the mirror.
10110{_}1505{_}She resolutely accompanied the detective, and Grant had to follow.
10110{_}1506{_}All three passed into the post office, Doris using the private door.
10110{_}1507{_}Mr. Martin looked up from his desk when they appeared, and requested his daughter to check a bundle of postal orders.
10110{_}1508{_}The pretext was painfully obvious, but Grant was not so wishful now to clear up matters with Doris's father, as the girl herself might be trusted to pass on an accurate account of the affair from beginning to end.
10110{_}1509{_}He was about to reach the street quick on Furneaux's heels when the little man turned suddenly.
10110{_}1510{_}"By the way, don't you want a shilling's worth of stamps?"
10110{_}1511{_}he said.
10110{_}1512{_}Grant smiled comprehension, and went back to the counter, where Doris herself served him.
10110{_}1513{_}She did not try to avoid his glance, but rather met it with a baffling serenity oddly at variance with her momentary loss of self-possession in the garden.
10110{_}1514{_}When he entered the street the detective had vanished.
10110{_}1515{_}He walked down the hill at a rapid pace, disregarding the eyes peeping at him through open doorways, over narrow window-curtains, and covertly staring when people passed in the roadway.
10110{_}1516{_}The sensitive side of his temperament shrank from this thinly-veiled hostility.
10110{_}1517{_}He was by way of being popular in Steynholme, yet not a soul spoke to him.
10110{_}1518{_}Before he reached the bridge, the other side of him, the man of action, of cool resource in an emergency, rose in rebellion against the league of silly clodhoppers.
10110{_}1519{_}Back he strode to the post office and dashed off a telegram.
10110{_}1520{_}It ran:  "Walter Hart, Savage Club, Adelphi, London.
10110{_}1521{_}Come here and help to lay a ghost."
10110{_}1522{_}He signed it in full, name and address.
10110{_}1523{_}Doris was gone, but her father received it, and read the text in a bewildered way.
10110{_}1524{_}"I find myself deserted by my Steynholme friends so I am trying to import one stanch one," said Grant, almost vindictively.
10110{_}1525{_}Martin murmured the cost, and Grant stormed out again.
10110{_}1526{_}This time, passing the Hare and Hounds, he looked at door and windows.
10110{_}1527{_}He caught a face scowling at him over a brown wire blind bearing the words "Wines and Spirits" on it in letters of dull gold.
10110{_}1528{_}It was a commonplace type of face, small-featured, ginger-moustached, and crowned by a billy-cock hat set at a rakish angle.
10110{_}1529{_}Its most marked characteristic was the positive hatred which glowed in the sharp, pale-blue eyes.
10110{_}1530{_}Grant wondered who this highly censorious young man might be.
10110{_}1531{_}At any rate, he meant to ascertain whether or not the critic was susceptible of satire at his own expense.
10110{_}1532{_}He walked up to the window, elevated his eyebrows at the frowning person within, pretended to read the words on the screen, looked again at the man inside, and shook his head gravely in the manner of one who has accurately determined cause and effect.
10110{_}1533{_}Fred Elkin was quick-witted enough to appreciate Grant's unspoken comment.
10110{_}1534{_}He was also unmannerly enough to put out his tongue.
10110{_}1535{_}Then Grant laughed, and turned on his heel.
10110{_}1536{_}Mr. Siddle, quietly observant of recent comings and goings, was standing at the door of the shop, and missed no item of this dumb show.
10110{_}1537{_}He raised both hands in silent condemnation of Elkin's childishness, whereupon the horse-dealer jerked a thumb toward Grant's retreating figure, and went through a rapid pantomime of the hanging process.
10110{_}1538{_}His crony disapproved again, and went in.
10110{_}1539{_}Now, both those men were on the jury panel, so, to all appearance, Grant would be judged by at least one deadly enemy, whose animosity might or might not be fairly balanced by the chemist's impartial mind.
10110{_}1540{_}The tenant of The Hollies actually dreaded the loneliness of his dwelling now, though it was that very quality which had drawn him to Steynholme a year earlier.
10110{_}1541{_}Work or reading was equally out of the question that day.
10110{_}1542{_}He sought the industrious Bates, who was trenching celery in the kitchen garden.
10110{_}1543{_}"Have 'ee made out owt about un, sir?"
10110{_}1544{_}inquired that hardy individual, pausing to spit on the handle of his spade.
10110{_}1545{_}"No," said Grant.
10110{_}1546{_}"The thing is a greater mystery than ever."
10110{_}1547{_}"I'm thinkin' her mun ha' bin killed by a loony," announced Bates.
10110{_}1548{_}"Something of the kind, no doubt.
10110{_}1549{_}But why are the little less dangerous loonies of Steynholme united in the belief that I am the guilty one?"
10110{_}1550{_}"Ax me another," growled Bates.
10110{_}1551{_}"Who is spreading this rumor?
10110{_}1552{_}Robinson?"
10110{_}1553{_}"'E dussen't, sir.
10110{_}1554{_}'E looks fierce, but 'e'll 'old 'is tongue.
10110{_}1555{_}T'super will see to that."
10110{_}1556{_}"Someone is talking.
10110{_}1557{_}That is quite certain."
10110{_}1558{_}"There's a chap in the 'Are an' 'Ounds--kem 'ere last night."
10110{_}1559{_}"Ingerman?"
10110{_}1560{_}"Ay, sir, that's the name.
10110{_}1561{_}'E's makin' a song of it, I hear."
10110{_}1562{_}"Anybody else?"
10110{_}1563{_}"Fred Elkin is gassin' about.
10110{_}1564{_}Do 'ee know un?
10110{_}1565{_}Breeds 'osses at Mount Farm, a mile that-a-way," and Bates pointed to the west.
10110{_}1566{_}Grant hazarded a guess, and described the face of condemnation seen at the inn.
10110{_}1567{_}Bates nodded.
10110{_}1568{_}"That's un," he said.
10110{_}1569{_}Then he drove the spade into the rich loam.
10110{_}1570{_}"They do say," he added, apparently as an after-thought, "as Fred Elkin is mighty sweet on Doris, but her'll 'ave nowt to do wi' un."
10110{_}1571{_}Grant whistled softly.
10110{_}1572{_}This explanation threw light on a dark place.
10110{_}1573{_}"The plot thickens," he said.
10110{_}1574{_}"Mr. Elkin becomes more interesting than he looks.
10110{_}1575{_}Are there other disappointed swains in the offing?"
10110{_}1576{_}"What's that, sir?"
10110{_}1577{_}"Has Miss Martin any other suitors?"
10110{_}1578{_}"Lots of 'em 'ud be after her like wasps round a plum-tree if she'd give 'em 'alf a chance.
10110{_}1579{_}But _you_ put a stopper on 'em."
10110{_}1580{_}Bates was blunt of speech, though a philosopher withal.
10110{_}1581{_}"Elkin is my only serious rival, then?"
10110{_}1582{_}laughed Grant, passing off as a joke a thrust which was shrewder than the gardener knew.
10110{_}1583{_}"'E 'as plenty of brass, but I reckon nowt on 'im," was the contemptuous answer.
10110{_}1584{_}"Well, he is not a likely person to kill a woman he had never before seen.
10110{_}1585{_}Miss Martin will marry whom she chooses, no doubt.
10110{_}1586{_}The present problem is to find out who murdered Miss Melhuish.
10110{_}1587{_}Now, had _I_ been the victim you would be thinking hard, Bates."
10110{_}1588{_}"I tell 'ee, sir, it wur a loony."
10110{_}1589{_}Nor was Bates to be moved from that opinion.
10110{_}1590{_}He held to it, through thick and thin, for many days.
10110{_}1591{_}Grant wandered into the front garden.
10110{_}1592{_}His eyes rose involuntarily to the distant post office, and he noticed at once that the dormer window was closed.
10110{_}1593{_}Yet Doris shared his own love of fresh air, and that window had always been open till that very hour.
10110{_}1594{_}Somehow, this simple thing seemed to shut him out of her life.
10110{_}1595{_}He walked to the river, and gazed at the spot where the body was drawn ashore.
10110{_}1596{_}In the absence of rain the water ran clear as gin, and the marks made by the feet of Adelaide Melhuish's murderer were still perceptible.
10110{_}1597{_}If only those misshapen blotches could reveal their secret!
10110{_}1598{_}If only some Heaven-sent ray of intuition would enable him to put the police on the track of the criminal!
10110{_}1599{_}Theoretically, a novelist and essayist should be a first-rate detective, yet, brought face to face with an actual felony, here was one who perforce remained blind and dumb.
10110{_}1600{_}Yet he was not blameworthy for failing to solve a mystery which was rapidly establishing a record for bewildering elements.
10110{_}1601{_}Wherein he did err most lamentably was in his reading of a woman's heart.
10110{_}1602{_}No answering telegram came from his friend in London.
10110{_}1603{_}The day wore slowly till it was time to attend the inquest.
10110{_}1604{_}He found a crowd gathered in front of the Hare and Hounds.
10110{_}1605{_}Superintendent Fowler was there, and quite a number of policemen, whose presence was explained when a buzz of excitement heralded Grant's arrival.
10110{_}1606{_}He decided not to stand this sort of persecution a moment longer.
10110{_}1607{_}Before the superintendent could interfere, he leaped on to a set of stone mounting-steps which stood opposite the door.
10110{_}1608{_}Instantly, seeing that he was about to speak, the angry murmuring of the mob was hushed.
10110{_}1609{_}He looked into a hundred stolid faces, and stretched out his right hand.
10110{_}1610{_}"I cannot help feeling," he said, in slow, incisive accents which carried far, "that a set of peculiar circumstances has led you Steynholme folk to suspect me of being responsible, in some way, for the death of the lady whose body was found in the river near my house.
10110{_}1611{_}Now, I want to tell you that I am not only an innocent but a much-maligned man.
10110{_}1612{_}The law of the land will establish both facts in due season.
10110{_}1613{_}But I want to warn some of you, too, I shall not trouble to issue writs for libel.
10110{_}1614{_}If any blackguard among you dares to insult me openly, I shall smash his face."
10110{_}1615{_}He knew when to stop.
10110{_}1616{_}Superintendent Fowler's nudge was not called for, as the orator simply met the scrutiny of all those eyes without another word.
10110{_}1617{_}Curiously enough, the sense of justice is inherent in every haphazard gathering of the public.
10110{_}1618{_}Grant's soldierly bearing, his calm defiance of hostile opinion, the outspoken threat which he so plainly meant, won instant favor.
10110{_}1619{_}Someone shouted, "Hear, hear!"
10110{_}1620{_}and the crowd applauded.
10110{_}1621{_}From that moment he had little to complain of in the attitude of the community as a whole.
10110{_}1622{_}There were subtle and dangerous enemies to be fought and conquered, but Steynholme looked on, keen to learn of any new sensation, of course, but placidly content that the final verdict should be left in the hands of the authorities.
10110{_}1623{_}CHAPTER VII  "ALARUMS AND EXCURSIONS"   The inquest was surprisingly tame after the stirring events which had led up to it.
10110{_}1624{_}Indeed, save for two incidents, the proceedings were almost dull.
10110{_}1625{_}The coroner, a Knoleworth solicitor named Belcher, prided himself on conducting this _cause celebre_ with as little ostentation as he would have displayed over an ordinary inquiry.
10110{_}1626{_}Messrs. Siddle, Elkin, Tomlin and Hobbs, with eight other local tradesmen and farmers, formed the jurors, and the chemist was promptly elected foreman; no witnesses were ordered out of court; the formalities of "swearing in" the jury and "viewing" the body were carried through rapidly.
10110{_}1627{_}Almost before Grant had time to assimilate these details Superintendent Fowler, who marshalled the evidence, called his name.
10110{_}1628{_}The coroner's officer tendered him a well-thumbed Bible, while the coroner himself administered the oath.
10110{_}1629{_}Grant eyed the somewhat soiled volume, and opened it before putting it to his lips.
10110{_}1630{_}The action probably did not please the jury.
10110{_}1631{_}Elkin nudged Tomlin, and sniggered at the rest of his colleagues, as much as to say: "What did I tell you?
10110{_}1632{_}The cheek of him!"
10110{_}1633{_}Elkin, by the way, looked ill.
10110{_}1634{_}When his interest flagged for an instant his haggard aspect became more noticeable.
10110{_}1635{_}Ingerman was there, of course.
10110{_}1636{_}Furneaux sat beside Mr. Fowler.
10110{_}1637{_}A stranger, whom Grant did not recognize, proved to be the County Chief Constable.
10110{_}1638{_}There was a strong muster of police, and the representatives of the press completely monopolized the scanty accommodation for the public.
10110{_}1639{_}To Grant's relief, Doris Martin was not in attendance.
10110{_}1640{_}He told the simple facts of the finding of Adelaide Melhuish's corpse.
10110{_}1641{_}A harmless question by the coroner evoked the first "scene" which set the reporters' pencils busy.
10110{_}1642{_}"Did you recognize the body!"
10110{_}1643{_}inquired Mr. Belcher.
10110{_}1644{_}"I did."
10110{_}1645{_}"Then you can give the jury her name?"
10110{_}1646{_}Before Grant could answer, Ingerman sprang up, his sallow face livid with passion.
10110{_}1647{_}"I protest, sir, against this man being permitted to identify my wife," he said.
10110{_}1648{_}He was either deeply moved, or proved himself an excellent actor.
10110{_}1649{_}His flute-like voice vibrated with an intense emotion.
10110{_}1650{_}Thus might Mark Antony have spoken when vowing that Brutus was an honorable man.
10110{_}1651{_}"Who are you?"
10110{_}1652{_}demanded the coroner sharply.
10110{_}1653{_}"Isidor George Ingerman, husband of the deceased lady," came the clear-toned reply.
10110{_}1654{_}"Well, sit down, sir, and do not interrupt the court again," said the coroner.
10110{_}1655{_}"I demand, sir, that you note my protest."
10110{_}1656{_}"Sit down!
10110{_}1657{_}Were you any other person I would have you removed.
10110{_}1658{_}As it is, I am prepared to regard your feelings to the extent of explaining that the witness is not identifying the body but relating a fact within his own knowledge."
10110{_}1659{_}Ingerman bowed, and resumed his seat.
10110{_}1660{_}For some reason, Grant stared blankly at Furneaux.
10110{_}1661{_}The latter did not meet his glance, but put a finger on those thin lips.
10110{_}1662{_}It might, or might not, be a warning to repress any retort he had in mind.
10110{_}1663{_}At any rate, obeying a nod from the coroner, he merely said:  "She was a well-known actress, Miss Adelaide Melhuish."
10110{_}1664{_}Mr. Belcher's pen hesitated a little.
10110{_}1665{_}Then it scratched on.
10110{_}1666{_}Undoubtedly, he was himself exercising the restraint he meant to impose on others.
10110{_}1667{_}"You are quite sure?"
10110{_}1668{_}he said, after a pause.
10110{_}1669{_}"Quite."
10110{_}1670{_}"Thank you, Mr. Grant.
10110{_}1671{_}Wait here until you sign your deposition.
10110{_}1672{_}Of course, you are aware that this inquiry will stand adjourned, and the whole matter will be gone into fully at a later date."
10110{_}1673{_}"So I have been informed, sir."
10110{_}1674{_}Ingerman was the next witness.
10110{_}1675{_}_He_, like a good democrat, kissed the cover of the Bible.
10110{_}1676{_}The coroner began by giving him some advice.
10110{_}1677{_}"This is a purely formal inquiry, to permit of a death certificate being issued.
10110{_}1678{_}You will oblige me, therefore, by answering my questions without introducing any extraneous subject."
10110{_}1679{_}Ingerman adhered to these instructions.
10110{_}1680{_}Having already shot a carefully-prepared bolt, he meant avoiding any further conflict with the authorities.
10110{_}1681{_}His evidence was brief and to the point.
10110{_}1682{_}The deceased was his wife.
10110{_}1683{_}They were married at a London registrar's office on a given date, six years ago.
10110{_}1684{_}His wife acted under her maiden name.
10110{_}1685{_}There was no family.
10110{_}1686{_}The court was well lighted by four long windows in the eastern wall, which each witness faced, so Grant was free to study his avowed enemy at leisure.
10110{_}1687{_}He thought he made out a crafty underlook in Ingerman which he had failed to detect the previous night.
10110{_}1688{_}That slow, smooth voice seemed to weigh each syllable.
10110{_}1689{_}Such a man would never blurt out an unconsidered admission.
10110{_}1690{_}He was a foe to be reckoned with.
10110{_}1691{_}The subtle malignancy of that well-timed outburst was proof positive in that respect.
10110{_}1692{_}The jury, apparently, attached much weight to his words.
10110{_}1693{_}On some faces there was an expectancy which merged into marked disappointment when his evidence came to an end.
10110{_}1694{_}The foreman alone displayed the judicial attitude warranted by the oath he had taken.
10110{_}1695{_}Somehow, Grant had faith in Mr. Siddle.
10110{_}1696{_}The man looked intellectual.
10110{_}1697{_}When spoken to in his shop his manner was invariably reserved.
10110{_}1698{_}But that was his general repute in Steynholme--a quiet, uninterfering person, who had come to the village a young man, yet had never really entered into its life.
10110{_}1699{_}For instance, he neither held nor would accept any public office.
10110{_}1700{_}At first, people wondered how he contrived to eke out a living, but this puzzle was solved by his admitted possession of a small annuity.
10110{_}1701{_}Dr. Foxton, general practitioner, who held undisputed sway in the district, told how he had conducted an autopsy on the body of the deceased.
10110{_}1702{_}He found a deep, incised wound on the back of the skull, a wound which would have caused death in any event.
10110{_}1703{_}The instrument used must have been a heavy and blunt one.
10110{_}1704{_}Miss Melhuish was dead or dying when thrown into the river.
10110{_}1705{_}The body was well nourished, and the vital organs sound.
10110{_}1706{_}Undoubtedly she had been murdered.
10110{_}1707{_}Bates followed, and evoked a snigger by the outspokenness of blunt Sussex.
10110{_}1708{_}"I hauled 'um in," he said, "an' knew it wur a dead 'un by the feel of the rope."
10110{_}1709{_}The coroner was not curious.
10110{_}1710{_}He merely wished to put on record the time and manner in which Mr. Grant summoned assistance.
10110{_}1711{_}Then P. C. Robinson entered the box, and contrived to bring about the second "incident."
10110{_}1712{_}He told how, "from information received," he went to The Hollies, and found Mr. Grant standing near the river with a dead body at his feet.
10110{_}1713{_}"One side of Mr. Grant's face was covered with blood," he went on.
10110{_}1714{_}If the policeman was minded to create a sensation, he certainly succeeded.
10110{_}1715{_}A slight hum ran through the court, and then all present seemed to restrain their breathing lest a word of the evidence should be lost.
10110{_}1716{_}The mention of "blood" in a murder case was a more adroit dodge than Robinson himself guessed, perhaps.
10110{_}1717{_}Few of his hearers troubled to reflect that a smudge of fresh gore on Grant's cheek could hardly have any bearing on the death of a woman whose body had admittedly lain all night in the river.
10110{_}1718{_}It sufficed that Robinson had introduced a touch of the right color into the inquiry.
10110{_}1719{_}Even the coroner was worried.
10110{_}1720{_}"Well!"
10110{_}1721{_}he said testily.
10110{_}1722{_}"I took down his statement, sir," said the witness, well knowing that he had wiped off Grant's morning score in the matter of Bush Walk.
10110{_}1723{_}"Never mind his statement.
10110{_}1724{_}That must await the adjourned hearing.
10110{_}1725{_}What did you do with the body?"
10110{_}1726{_}"Took it to the stable of the Hare and Hounds, sir."
10110{_}1727{_}"Where it was viewed recently by the jury?"
10110{_}1728{_}"Yes, sir."
10110{_}1729{_}"It is the body identified by Mr. Ingerman as that of his wife?"
10110{_}1730{_}"Yes, sir."
10110{_}1731{_}"That will do....
10110{_}1732{_}Superintendent Fowler, will this day week at ten o'clock suit you?"
10110{_}1733{_}"Yes, sir," said the superintendent.
10110{_}1734{_}"Then the inquest stands adjourned until that day and hour.
10110{_}1735{_}Gentlemen of the jury, you must be here punctually."
10110{_}1736{_}"Can't we ask any questions?"
10110{_}1737{_}cried Elkin, in an injured tone.
10110{_}1738{_}"No.
10110{_}1739{_}You cannot," snapped the coroner emphatically.
10110{_}1740{_}After a few formalities, which included the reading and signing of the depositions, the courthouse emptied.
10110{_}1741{_}The whole thing was over in half an hour.
10110{_}1742{_}Grant, determined to have a word with the representative of Scotland Yard, went openly to Furneaux, and asked him to come to The Hollies and join him in a cup of tea.
10110{_}1743{_}"No," was the curt answer.
10110{_}1744{_}"I'm busy.
10110{_}1745{_}I'll see you later."
10110{_}1746{_}It was difficult to reconcile the detective's present stand-off manner with his earlier camaradie, to say nothing of the seemingly friendly hint conveyed by the signal to pass no comment on Ingerman's interruption.
10110{_}1747{_}Rather sick at heart, Grant went out into the sunshine.
10110{_}1748{_}He was snap-shotted a dozen times by press photographers.
10110{_}1749{_}One man, backing impudently in front of him in order to secure a sharp focus, tripped over the raised edge of a cartway into a yard, and sat down violently.
10110{_}1750{_}The onlookers laughed, but Grant helped the photographer to rise.
10110{_}1751{_}"If you want a really good picture of the Steynholme murderer, come to my place, and I'll give you one," he said.
10110{_}1752{_}The pressman was grateful, because Grant's action had tended to mitigate his discomfiture.
10110{_}1753{_}"No one but a fool thinks of you as a murderer, Mr. Grant," he said.
10110{_}1754{_}"What I really want is a portrait of 'the celebrated' author in whose grounds the body was found."
10110{_}1755{_}"Come along, then, and I'll pose for you."
10110{_}1756{_}The photographer was surprised, but joyfully accepted the gifts the gods gave.
10110{_}1757{_}He could not guess that his host was pining for human companionship.
10110{_}1758{_}He could not fathom Grant's disappointment, on reaching The Hollies, at finding no telegram from a trusted friend, Walter Hart.
10110{_}1759{_}And he was equally unconscious of the immense service he rendered by compelling his host to talk and act naturally.
10110{_}1760{_}He enlightened Grant, too, in the matter of inquests.
